matter

Yes, the Octopus Is Smart as Heck. But Why?

It has eight arms, three hearts — and a plan. Scientists aren’t sure how the cephalopods got to be so intelligent.

Image
An octopus in an aquarium in Brest, France. The animals are highly intelligent, but researchers are uncertain how the trait evolved. CreditCreditFred Tanneau/Agence France-Presse — Getty Images

By Carl Zimmer

  • Nov. 30, 2018

To demonstrate how smart an octopus can be, Piero Amodio points to a YouTube video. It shows an octopus pulling two halves of a coconut shell together to hide inside. Later the animal stacks the shells together like nesting bowls — and carts them away.

“It suggests the octopus is carrying these tools around because it has some understanding they may be useful in the future,” said Mr. Amodio, a graduate student studying animal intelligence at the University of Cambridge in Britain.

&lt;/div&gt;&lt;figcaption itemprop=&quot;caption description&quot; class=&quot;css-109u0hy emkp2hg0&quot; /&gt;&lt;/figure&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn&quot;&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;css-4w7y5l&quot;&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;But his amazement is mixed with puzzlement. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;For decades, researchers have studied how certain animals evolved to be intelligent, among them apes, elephants, dolphins and even some birds, such as crows and parrots. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;aside class=&quot;css-1m2ozyi&quot;&gt;&lt;span /&gt;&lt;/aside&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn&quot;&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;css-4w7y5l&quot;&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;But all the scientific theories fail when it comes to cephalopods, a group that includes octopuses, squid and cuttlefish. Despite feats of creativity, they &lt;a class=&quot;css-1g7m0tk&quot; href=&quot;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.tree.2018.10.010&quot; title=&quot;&quot; rel=&quot;noopener noreferrer&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;lack some hallmarks&lt;/a&gt; of intelligence seen in other species. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;“It’s an apparent paradox that’s been largely overlooked in the past,” said Mr. Amodio. He and five other experts on animal intelligence explore this paradox in a paper published this month in the journal Trends in Ecology and Evolution. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;For scientists who study animal behavior, intelligence is not about acing a calculus test or taking a car apart and putting it back together. Intelligence comprises sophisticated cognitive skills that help an animal thrive. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;That may include the ability to come up with solutions to the problem of finding food, for example, or a knack for planning for some challenge in the future. Intelligent animals don’t rely on fixed responses to survive — they can invent new behaviors on the fly.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;To measure animal intelligence, scientists observe creatures in the wild — watching a dolphin &lt;a class=&quot;css-1g7m0tk&quot; href=&quot;https://www.nytimes.com/2011/07/26/science/26obdolphin.html?module=inline&quot; title=&quot;&quot;&gt;stick a sponge on its beak to avoid getting cuts&lt;/a&gt; from sharp rocks and coral, for example. Or they bring animals into the lab and offer them puzzles to solve, such as rewarding crows when they &lt;a class=&quot;css-1g7m0tk&quot; href=&quot;https://www.nytimes.com/2018/06/28/science/crows-toolmaking.html?module=inline&quot; title=&quot;&quot;&gt;learn to rip paper into strips of just the right size&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;aside class=&quot;css-1m2ozyi&quot;&gt;&lt;span /&gt;&lt;/aside&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn&quot;&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;css-4w7y5l&quot;&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;Only a few species stand out in these studies, and by comparing them, scientists have identified some shared factors. The animals have big brains relative to their body size, they live for a long time, and they can form long-lasting social bonds.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;Those similarities have led to some promising explanations for how certain animals evolved to be smart.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;One is known as the ecological intelligence hypothesis. It holds that intelligence evolves as an adaptation for finding food. While some animals have a reliable food supply, others have to cope with unpredictability.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;“If you eat fruit, you have to remember where the fruiting trees are and when they’re ripe,” said Mr. Amadio. “It can be much more cognitively challenging than eating leaves.”&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;Tools allow animals to get to food that they couldn’t reach otherwise. And if they can make plans for the future, they can store food to survive hard times.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;Other researchers have argued for what’s known as the social intelligence hypothesis: Smarter animals “cooperate and learn from other members of the same species,” said Mr. Amadio.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;Together, these forces appear to have encouraged the development of bigger, more powerful brains. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;Smart animals also tend to live for a long time, and it’s possible that bigger brains drove the evolution of longevity. It takes years for juveniles to develop these complex organs, during which time they need help from adults to get enough food.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;aside class=&quot;css-1m2ozyi&quot;&gt;&lt;span /&gt;&lt;/aside&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn&quot;&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;css-4w7y5l&quot;&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;Cephalopods behave in ways that certainly suggest they’re highly intelligent. An octopus named Inky, for example, made a notorious escape recently from the National Aquarium of New Zealand, exiting his enclosure and slithering into a floor drain and, apparently, out to sea. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;Cuttlefish can scare off predators by forming eyespots on their bodies in order to look like giant fish. But they only use this trick against predators that rely on vision to find prey. If a predator that depends on smell shows up, the cuttlefish are smart enough just to flee.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;Octopuses show the same flexibility when scientists bring them into labs. In one study, researchers at Hebrew University presented octopuses with an L-shaped box with food inside. The animals &lt;a class=&quot;css-1g7m0tk&quot; href=&quot;https://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371/journal.pone.0152048&quot; title=&quot;&quot; rel=&quot;noopener noreferrer&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;figured out how to push and pull the morsel through a tiny hole&lt;/a&gt; in the wall of their tank. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;Another feature that cephalopods share with other smart animals is a relatively big brain. But that’s where the similarities appear to end. Most of the neurons that do the computing, for example, are in the octopus’s arms. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;&lt;strong class=&quot;css-8qgvsz euv7paa0&quot;&gt;&lt;em class=&quot;css-2fg4z9 ehxkw330&quot;&gt;[&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;strong class=&quot;css-8qgvsz euv7paa0&quot;&gt;&lt;a class=&quot;css-1g7m0tk&quot; href=&quot;http://on.fb.me/1paTQ1h&quot; title=&quot;&quot; rel=&quot;noopener noreferrer&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;&lt;em class=&quot;css-2fg4z9 ehxkw330&quot;&gt;Like the Science Times page on Facebook.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;strong class=&quot;css-8qgvsz euv7paa0&quot;&gt;&lt;em class=&quot;css-2fg4z9 ehxkw330&quot;&gt; &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;em class=&quot;css-2fg4z9 ehxkw330&quot;&gt;| Sign up for the &lt;/em&gt;&lt;strong class=&quot;css-8qgvsz euv7paa0&quot;&gt;&lt;a class=&quot;css-1g7m0tk&quot; href=&quot;http://nyti.ms/1MbHaRU&quot; title=&quot;&quot; rel=&quot;noopener noreferrer&quot; target=&quot;_blank&quot;&gt;&lt;em class=&quot;css-2fg4z9 ehxkw330&quot;&gt;Science Times newsletter.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;strong class=&quot;css-8qgvsz euv7paa0&quot;&gt;&lt;em class=&quot;css-2fg4z9 ehxkw330&quot;&gt;]&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;Most strikingly, cephalopods die young. Some may live as long as two years, while others only last a few months. Nor do cephalopods form social bonds. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;They get together to mate, but males and females don’t stay together for long or care for their young. While chimpanzees and dolphins may live in societies of dozens of other animals, cephalopods seem to be loners.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;Mr. Amodio and his colleagues think the evolutionary history of cephalopods may explain this intelligence paradox. About half a billion years ago, their snaillike ancestors evolved to use their shells as a buoyancy device. They could load chambers in the shell with gas to float up and down in the ocean.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;aside class=&quot;css-1m2ozyi&quot;&gt;&lt;span /&gt;&lt;/aside&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn&quot;&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;css-4w7y5l&quot;&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;A cousin of cephalopods, the nautilus, still lives this way. Like cephalopods, it has tentacles. It also has a somewhat enlarged brain, although it doesn’t seem to be anywhere as intelligent as an octopus.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;About 275 million years ago, the ancestor of today’s cephalopods lost the external shell. It’s not clear why, but it must have been liberating. Now the animals could start exploring places that had been off-limits to their shelled ancestors. Octopuses could slip into rocky crevices, for example, to hunt for prey.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;On the other hand, losing their shells left cephalopods quite vulnerable to hungry predators. This threat may have driven cephalopods to become masters of disguise and escape. They did so by evolving big brains, the ability to solve new problems, and perhaps look into the future — knowing that coconut or clam shells may come in handy, for example.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;aside class=&quot;css-1m2ozyi&quot;&gt;&lt;span /&gt;&lt;/aside&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div data-testid=&quot;photoviewer-wrapper&quot; class=&quot;css-79elbk ehw59r11&quot;&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;css-z3e15g&quot; data-testid=&quot;photoviewer-wrapper-hidden&quot; /&gt;&lt;div data-testid=&quot;photoviewer-children&quot; class=&quot;css-1a48zt4 ehw59r111&quot;&gt;&lt;figure class=&quot;css-1lghxe1 e1a8i6eb0&quot; src=&quot;https://static01.nyt.com/images/2018/12/04/science/04SCI-ZIMMER-02/merlin_147528999_8c2f3373-6d00-4b38-b113-f485f9f3a9a8-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;amp;auto=webp&amp;amp;disable=upscale&quot; /&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;figcaption itemprop=&quot;caption description&quot; class=&quot;css-1l44abu e3zkro30&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;css-8i9d0s e1olku6u0&quot;&gt;An octopus pulls together two empty shells in order to hide off the coast of Sulawesi, Indonesia.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span itemprop=&quot;copyrightHolder&quot; class=&quot;css-vuqh7u e18m0s9i0&quot;&gt;&lt;span class=&quot;css-1ly73wi e1afaoz0&quot;&gt;Credit&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span&gt;Ethan Daniels, via Getty Images&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/figcaption&gt;&lt;/figure&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;css-u5vfum StoryBodyCompanionColumn&quot;&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;css-4w7y5l&quot;&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;Yet intelligence is not the perfect solution for cephalopods, Mr. Amodio suggested. Sooner or later, they get eaten. Natural selection has turned them into a paradox: a short-lived, intelligent animal.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;Mr. Amodio said that scientists still need to learn a lot more about cephalopods before they can know if this hypothesis is sound. But the research may do more than shine a light on octopuses and their cousins: It could give us a deeper understanding of intelligence in general.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&quot;css-1ygdjhk e2kc3sl0&quot;&gt;“We can’t take for granted that there’s just one way to intelligence,” Mr. Amodio said. “There could be different paths.”&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;aside class=&quot;css-1m2ozyi&quot;&gt;&lt;span /&gt;&lt;/aside&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;css-1ur45q4 e5d2tgl3&quot;&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;css-1ukm2ij e5d2tgl0&quot;&gt;More octopus articles you might like&lt;/div&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;css-15g2oxy e5d2tgl2&quot;&gt;&lt;div class=&quot;css-egqive ezm5mny6&quot;&gt;&lt;a class=&quot;css-1g7m0tk&quot;
